    </p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
      30th August 2021
    </span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 9th October 2021</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 11th October 2021</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>In comparison with lithium-ion batteries, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been proposed as an alternative for large-scale energy storage. However, finding an anode material that can overcome the sluggish electrochemical reaction kinetics and fast capacity fading caused by large volume expansion during cycling is problematic. In this study, the intercalation technique for nitrogen-doped carbon layers is implemented for the molybdenum disulfide (MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC) structure to improve the rate and cycling stability of SIBs by increasing the diffusion rate of sodium ions and mitigating excessive volume structural expansion. The as-synthesized MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC anode has a high discharge specific capacity of 546 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 160 cycles, as well as a high rate and stable cycle performance of 406 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 10 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 1000 cycles. Upon coupling with a high-voltage Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>F cathode, the sodium-ion full battery displays high specific energies of 78.57 W h kg<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and 49.70 W h kg<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at specific powers of 193.76 W kg<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and 3756.80 W kg<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively, with commercialization potential demonstrated.</p></div><hr>
    
      
      <h2 id="sect298"><span class="a_heading">1. Introduction</span></h2>
      <span>Despite the fact that lithium ion batteries (LIBs) have been successfully commercialized, with the massive growth of the new energy vehicle industry, demand for lithium batteries is rapidly increasing, and lithium resources are in an exceptional state of crisis.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1–11</span></sup></a> Therefore, developing an alternative renewable energy storage technology is critical. Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have a commercial advantage over lithium-ion batteries since the raw material is abundant and inexpensive. Lithium and sodium, on the other hand, belong to the same major group and have similar electrochemical activity during the charge and discharge process. Two-dimensional transition metal dichalcogenides (2D-TMD) have recently risen to prominence as anode materials in SIBs research owing to their unique physical structure and chemical properties.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">12–14</span></sup></a></span>
      <p class="otherpara">Molybdenum sulfide (MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>) demonstrates a precise multilayer morphology depicting three-atom stacked layers (S–Mo–S) that are bounded collectively <span class="italic">via</span> van der Waals interactions.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit15"><sup><span class="sup_ref">15–17</span></sup></a> Such morphology allows sodium ions to be incorporated as well as separated along the parallel plane. The perpendicular development of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanosheets onto the graphene substrate facilitates the uncovering of more active sites when applied to electrochemical applications which consequently creates an open channel for the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> intercalation process to occur resulting in improved and enhanced sodium storage.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit18"><sup><span class="sup_ref">18</span></sup></a> For instance, Teng <span class="italic">et al.</span> have devised a nanostructure with a vertical MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> orientation that is fixed on graphene sheets for the application of LIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit19"><sup><span class="sup_ref">19</span></sup></a> The transfer of Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> with its respective electrochemical reaction has been greatly improved because of the increased available active sites that are present.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit20"><sup><span class="sup_ref">20</span></sup></a> Nonetheless, the formation of covalent bonds at the graphene–MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> interface is predicted to improve the rate of reaction and simultaneously sustain the structural integrity.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit21"><sup><span class="sup_ref">21</span></sup></a> In such an aspect, controlling the reaction process is crucial in attaining beneficial growth and direct bonding between graphene and MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and thus facilitates the successful optimization of the as-designed electrode.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">A highly stable expanded interlayer architecture must be developed to enhance the capacity, rate performance, and cycling stability of SIBs. In this work, we have adopted an efficient hydrothermal strategy followed by thermal decomposition to prepare molybdenum disulfide and nitrogen-doped carbon (MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC) hybrid layered structures having an expanded interlayer spacing of 0.70 nm after integrating an N-doped carbon layer in MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> layers. The MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC hybrid exhibits a high discharge capacity of 546 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 160 cycles, a superb rate and a stable cycle capacity of 406 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 10 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 1000 cycles. An MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC//Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>F sodium-ion full battery also shows high specific energies of 78.57 W h kg<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and 49.70 W h kg<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at specific powers of 193.76 W kg<small><sup>−1</sup></small>and 3756.80 W kg<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively. The excellent performance makes the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC hybrid a competitive anode for SIBs.</p>
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect338"><span class="a_heading">2. Experimental section</span></h2>
      
        <h3 id="sect341"><span class="b_heading">Preparation of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC</span></h3>
        <span>1 mmol of sodium molybdate dihydrate (Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>MoO<small><sub>4</sub></small>·2H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O, 0.2419 g) and 4 mmol of <span class="small_caps">L</span>-cysteine (C<small><sub>3</sub></small>H<small><sub>7</sub></small>NO<small><sub>2</sub></small>S, 0.4846 g) were dissolved in 30 mL of deionized water and vigorously stirred at room temperature for 30 min. 100 mg of melamine was then added to the above solution and stirred for another 30 minutes. The prepared homogeneous solution was then transferred to a 50 mL Teflon-lined stainless steel autoclave and maintained at 200 °C for 24 h. The black precipitate denoted as MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C<small><sub>3</sub></small>H<small><sub>6</sub></small>N<small><sub>6</sub></small> was washed several times by centrifugation with deionized water and ethanol. It was finally calcinated in nitrogen at 500 °C for 2 h, and the heating rate was maintained at 5 °C min<small><sup>−1</sup></small> in order to obtain MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC.</span>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect358"><span class="b_heading">Preparation of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small></span></h3>
        <span>Pristine MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> was synthesized as the control sample similar to MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC with the exception of melamine.</span>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect364"><span class="b_heading">Preparation of Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>F</span></h3>
        <span>1 mmol of V<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>5</sub></small> and 3 mmol of H<small><sub>2</sub></small>C<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>4</sub></small> were firstly dissolved in deionized water under stirring at 70 °C for 1 hour. Subsequently, certain amounts of NH<small><sub>4</sub></small>H<small><sub>2</sub></small>PO<small><sub>4</sub></small> and NaF were added into the former solution, the pH value of which was adjusted to 7.00 using ammonia. The prepared homogeneous solution was then transferred to a 50 mL Teflon-lined stainless steel autoclave and maintained at 170 °C for 12 h. Finally, after washing several times by centrifugation with deionized water and drying in a vacuum oven at 80 °C, Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>F was collected.</span>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect385"><span class="b_heading">Material characterization</span></h3>
        <span>X-ray diffraction (XRD, Bruker, D/MAX-2000) with Cu Kα radiation was performed for the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC composite. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) using Hitachi SU-9010, transmission electron microscopy (TEM), energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS), high-resolution (HR)-TEM and selected area electron diffraction (SAED) using Fei Tecani G-20 were carried out to determine the morphology and elemental composition of samples. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was carried out with Al Kα as the excitation source (Thermo Fisher Scientific, ESCALAB 250XI). Raman spectra were obtained with a laser excitement of 532 nm using an HR-Evolution device from Horiba Jobin Yvon Co. The thermal analysis techniques (TG-DSC) test was carried out using a TG/DTA-7300 system from Seiko Instruments Inc., Japan. An ASAP 2020 automatic physisorption analyzer was used to obtain the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) curves (Micromeritics, USA).</span>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect389"><span class="b_heading">Electrochemical measurements</span></h3>
        <span>Polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) was blended to obtain the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode, which contains twice the mass of Super P, seven times the mass of the as-prepared materials and some <span class="italic">N</span>-methylpyrrolidone (NMP). Then the slurry was uniformly spread out on Cu foil and dried for 12 h at 120 °C in a vacuum oven. All of the electrodes were assembled simultaneously in a glove box containing highly pure argon gas (O<small><sub>2</sub></small> and H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O levels &lt; 0.01 ppm) as CR2032 coin-types to test their electrochemical performance. The counter electrode was Na foil, and the separator was a polypropylene film (glass microfiber filters). 1 M NaPF<small><sub>6</sub></small> dissolved in dimethyl ether (DME) was used as the electrolyte. Within a voltage window of 0.01–3.0 V, the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT), galvanostatic, and rate charge/discharge measurements were performed. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) and cyclic voltammetry (CV) were also carried out on a CHI660E electrochemical workstation. The MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC anode is coupled with the Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>F cathode with a weight ratio of 1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">(4.5–5). Before full cell assembly, the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC anode was first presodiated in a half-cell configuration for five discharge/charge cycles to form a stable SEI layer. The full cell voltage window was performed from 1.25 to 3.75 V.</span>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect404"><span class="b_heading">Computational measurements</span></h3>
        <span>DMol<small><sub>3</sub></small> in Materials Studio (v7.0) was used to perform density functional theory (DFT) calculations.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit22"><sup><span class="sup_ref">22–24</span></sup></a> The Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof (PBE) functional was used to model the electronic exchange and correlation effects.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit25"><sup><span class="sup_ref">25</span></sup></a> The basis set was the DNP (dual numerical atomic orbital with polarized p-function). Grimme's scheme (DFT-D2) was used to account for weak interactions.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit26"><sup><span class="sup_ref">26</span></sup></a> The threshold values for convergence of energies, gradient and displacement during geometry optimization were 1 × 10<small><sup>−5</sup></small> Ha, 1 × 10<small><sup>−3</sup></small> Ha Å<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, and 5 × 10<small><sup>−3</sup></small> Å, respectively. <span class="italic">k</span>-Point meshes of 17 × 17 × 1 and 13 × 13 × 1 configurations were applied to optimize the unit cells of graphene and MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> (2H phase), respectively. The MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/graphene heterostructure was constructed using a MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> 3 × 3 supercell and a graphene 4 × 4 supercell. A bilayer MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> (BL-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>) with a 3 × 3 supercell was adopted for comparison. The <span class="italic">k</span>-point mesh was 4 × 4 × 1 during the optimization of the BL-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> 3 × 3 supercell and the heterostructure.</span>
        <p class="otherpara">The adsorption energy (<span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ads</sub></small>) for a single Na atom on BL-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and the heterostructure is defined as:<br></p><table><tbody><tr><td class="eqnText"><span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ads</sub></small> = <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>Na*</sub></small> − <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>Na</sub></small> − <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>*</sub></small></td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>Na*</sub></small> represents the total energy of the optimized adsorption system, <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>*</sub></small> denotes the energy of the adsorbent, and <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>Na</sub></small> denotes the energy per atom in bulk Na. To determine the diffusion barriers (<span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>b</sub></small>) of adsorbed Na on different surfaces, transition state searches were conducted using the complete linear synchronous transit/quadratic synchronous transit (LST/QST) method.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit27"><sup><span class="sup_ref">27</span></sup></a><p></p>
      
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect445"><span class="a_heading">3. Results and discussion</span></h2>
      <span>As is illustrated in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1a</a>, sodium molybdate dihydrate was used as the source of molybdenum, <span class="small_caps">L</span>-cysteine was used as the source of sulfur, and melamine was used as the intercalator to introduce carbon and nitrogen. Melamine was inserted <span class="italic">via</span> a one-step hydrothermal process. By increasing the crystalline nature of the material through subsequent thermal decomposition, the final composite MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC has been achieved. In order to analyze its structure and crystallinity, X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns were obtained for MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC and pure MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1b</a>. The two product diffraction peaks are consistent with those of pristine 2H MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> (JCPDS #37-1492). The four main diffraction peaks for the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC composite are at 2<span class="italic">θ</span> values of 12.6°, 33.5°, 58.3°, and 70.1° and correspond to the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> planes (002), (101), (110), and (108), respectively, showing a high purity of the product. It is also worth noting that the diffraction peak corresponding to the (002) crystal plane of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC has a slight left shift compared to pristine MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, indicating an increase in the interlayer spacing. This could be due to the introduction of melamine for carbonized N-doped carbon layer intercalation increasing the interplanar spacing between MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC layers. The <span class="italic">d</span>-spacing values between MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC and pure MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> layers are calculated to be 0.702 and 0.620 nm, respectively.</span>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2021/NR/d1nr05708a/d1nr05708a-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d1nr05708a-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2021/NR/d1nr05708a/d1nr05708a-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Schematic diagram of the synthesis processes of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC sample. (b) XRD pattern of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC and pristine MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>. (c) Raman spectrum of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC sample. High-resolution (HR) XPS spectra of (d) Mo 3d, (e) S 2p, (f) N 1s and (g) C 1s for the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC sample, respectively.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">The above result agrees with the following high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) measurement, implying that N-doped carbon insertion between MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> layers was successful. The Raman spectra of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1c</a>, clearly show the D peak (1352 cm<small><sup>−1</sup></small>) for amorphous carbon and the G peak (1572 cm<small><sup>−1</sup></small>) for graphite carbon, confirming the presence of the carbon material. The D peak is higher than the G peak, indicating that the carbon in the material is mostly amorphous. Furthermore, the thermogravimetric (TG) curves show a distinct weight loss of 19.6% accompanied by a continuous endothermal process from 200 to 400 °C, indicating carbon decomposition in the material and oxidation of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> to MoO<small><sub>3</sub></small> in the air. Furthermore, there is a 2.2% weight loss below 200 °C, which can be attributed to the volatilization of absorbed water molecules. The accurate loading of carbon in MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC was calculated, and the mass percentage of carbon in MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC is 7.79% (Fig. S1<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). The nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC were measured and are shown in Fig. S2,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> indicating the existence of a porous structure. The specific surface area of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC is as high as 77 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. The large specific surface area of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC due to its two-dimensional lamellar structure can enlarge the contact area between the electrolyte and the electrode, which could endow more active sites to facilitate the storage of sodium ions.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was used to investigate the chemical composition and element valence states of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC composite. The XPS survey spectrum of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC is shown in Fig. S3,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> where the signals of Mo, S, C, and N are clearly visible. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1d and e</a> show the XPS core spectra for Mo 3d and S 2p, which show the typical Mo and S element peaks respectively. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1d</a>, Mo 3d<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and Mo 3d<small><sub>5/2</sub></small> are assigned to the two peaks at 232.6 and 229.5 eV, respectively. The Mo<small><sup>6+</sup></small> and S 2s can be attributed to the peaks at 235.9 eV and 226.7 eV.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit28"><sup><span class="sup_ref">28–33</span></sup></a> The peaks at 162.3 eV and 163.5 eV corresponding to S 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and S 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small> in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1e</a> are attributed to the presence of S<small><sup>2−</sup></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit31"><sup><span class="sup_ref">31,32,34,35</span></sup></a> The XPS spectrum for the N 1s core-level is divided into two parts, pyridinic N (398.1 eV) and graphitic N (401.8 eV) respectively, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1f</a>.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit36"><sup><span class="sup_ref">36,37</span></sup></a> Furthermore, the peaks at 284.3, 285.0, 286.0 and 288.8 eV correspond to the C–C, C–N, C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">N and C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O bonds, respectively, for the high-resolution C 1s XPS spectrum (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1g</a>).<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit38"><sup><span class="sup_ref">38,39</span></sup></a> The morphology and microstructure of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC have been studied further by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). In contrast to SEM and TEM of pure aggregate MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> (Fig. S4a and b<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>), the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC composite in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a–c</a> shows a continuous network of two-dimensional (2D) layers. In a dimension of around 60–100 nm, the multiple lamellar layers are partially stacked. It can be deduced from the corresponding selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2e</a> that the two-dimensional lamellar structure is MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, which is consistent with previous reports. The HRTEM images of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC and pure MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2d</a> and S4c,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> respectively. The lattice fringes with a <span class="italic">d</span>-spacing of 0.70 nm, corresponding to the (002) crystal face of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, which is larger than that of pure MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2d</a>. Furthermore, the high-angle annular dark-field and scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) and energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) elemental mapping images in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2f</a> clearly show the uniform distribution of Mo, S, N, and C elements throughout the hierarchical structure and also indicate that the N-doped carbon layer is successfully intercalated. These results indicate that the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC composite is successfully prepared. The intercalation of N-doped carbon and the hierarchical structure may play a key role in improving the Na ion storage performance.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2021/NR/d1nr05708a/d1nr05708a-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d1nr05708a-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2021/NR/d1nr05708a/d1nr05708a-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">Morphology characterization. (a) SEM image; TEM images with (b) low and (c) high magnifications; (d) HRTEM image; (e) SAED image; and (f) HAADF-STEM image and the corresponding EDS elemental mapping images of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC sample.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">The electrochemical performances of an Na-ion half-cell with MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC as the negative material were tested to investigate the storage behavior. The CV curves of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode in the potential range of 0.01–3.0 V were analyzed at a scanning rate of 0.1 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (see <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a</a>). The first discharge process has two cathodic peaks in the first cycle. The peaks at 1.07 V and 0.30 V represent the sodiation process and the formation of Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>S and the SEI film.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit33"><sup><span class="sup_ref">33,40–46</span></sup></a> The first anodic curve has a peak at 1.76 V corresponding to the desodiation process. The second and third curves almost overlap, demonstrating that the electrochemical reactions are highly reversible. The galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of the 1<small><sup>st</sup></small> cycle to the 5<small><sup>th</sup></small> cycle of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3b</a>. The capacities for charging and discharging in the 1<small><sup>st</sup></small> cycle are 922 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and 747 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at a current density of 0.2 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively, which demonstrates an 81% initial coulombic efficiency (ICE). As a comparison, the galvanostatic cycling performances of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC hybrid and pristine MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3c</a>. The specific capacity of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode has remained stable at the 160<small><sup>th</sup></small> cycle and slightly increases to 546 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> whereas the pristine MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> electrode has a lower specific capacity of 380 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at the same current density of 1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3d</a> shows that the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode is capable of achieving reversible capacities of 654, 607, 564, 513, 443, and 385 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at current densities of 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 5.0, and 10.0 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively. Furthermore, throughout the charging and discharging of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC and pristine MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> electrodes at different current densities in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3e</a>, the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode shows higher specific capacity as compared to the pristine MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> electrode with a nearly 100% CE. For comparison purposes, the as-prepared MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode is compared with other recently reported materials at various current densities (see <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3f</a>) and it exhibits a specific capacity superior to those of the other materials.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit16"><sup><span class="sup_ref">16,28,33,34,47–51</span></sup></a><a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3g</a> shows that the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode has a high specific capacity of 406 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at an elevated current density of 10 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and its specific capacity can be maintained after 1000 cycles without significant decay, showing the cycling stability of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode. These excellent performances of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode can be attributed to its customized beneficial features of expanded interlayer spacing and interconnection lamellar layers of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and NC sheets that can improve the sodiation capacity and strengthen the structural integrity of electrode materials.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2021/NR/d1nr05708a/d1nr05708a-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d1nr05708a-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2021/NR/d1nr05708a/d1nr05708a-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">(a) CV curves collected at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small> over the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V; (b) charging–discharging curves at 0.2 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> between 0.01 and 3 V for the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode; and (c) cycling performances of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode <span class="italic">versus</span> a pristine MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> electrode. (d) Charging/discharging curves of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode at current densities from 0.1 to 10 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and (e) corresponding cycling profiles of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode <span class="italic">versus</span> a pristine MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> electrode; (f) comparison of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode and other previously reported MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-based electrodes at various current densities; and (g) long cycling performance of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode at 10 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> for 1000 cycles.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">To further investigate the electrochemical performances of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode, cyclic voltammetry (CV) was conducted at scan rates of 0.1 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small> to 3.0 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4a</a>). The redox peaks increase with the scan rate, implying that the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode has a good rate capability. The Na ion storage mechanism can be deduced by applying the following formulas <a title="" href="#eqn1">(1) and (2)</a>:</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn1"><span id="eqn1"><span class="italic">i</span> = <span class="italic">av</span><small><sup><span class="italic">b</span></sup></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(1)</td></tr></tbody></table><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn2"><span id="eqn2">log(<span class="italic">i</span>) = <span class="italic">b</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">log(<span class="italic">v</span>) + log(<span class="italic">a</span>)</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(2)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">i</span> is the peak current, <span class="italic">v</span> is the scanning rate (mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>), and <span class="italic">a</span> and <span class="italic">b</span> are constants. Diffusion or pseudocapacitance behavior was used to control the charging/discharging processes in the SIBs that can be determined by the slope of the fitted lines (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4b</a>). This means that when the slope is 0.5, the process is controlled by diffusion, whereas when the slope approaches 1.0, pseudocapacitance behavior controls the process. The slope values of peaks 1, 2, 3 and 4 are estimated to be 1.17, 0.86, 1.09 and 1.02, respectively, for the oxidation process. It is evident that during the sodiation and desodiation processes of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode, the kinetic process is controlled by pseudocapacitance. The capacity contribution at various scan rates is assessed using the following formula <a title="" href="#eqn3">(3)</a>:<table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn3"><span id="eqn3"><span class="italic">i</span> = <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>1</sub></small><span class="italic">v</span> + <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>2</sub></small><span class="italic">v</span><small><sup>0.5</sup></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(3)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> and <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>2</sub></small> remain constant in the same electrochemical reaction. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4c</a> shows that at the scan rates of 0.6, 0.8 and 1.0 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, the contribution of pseudocapacitance accounts for 84.34%, 86.47% and 87.77% of the total capacity, respectively. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4d</a> and S5,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> with the scan rate increasing from 0.1 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small> to 3.0 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, the proportion of pseudocapacitive contribution raised from 70.88% to 93.15%. This result further concludes that the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode is controlled by pseudocapacitance behavior, which may be related to its characteristic crystalline structure and large specific surface area. Such a high ratio of pseudocapacitance control can accelerate the electrochemical kinetics reaction.<p></p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2021/NR/d1nr05708a/d1nr05708a-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d1nr05708a-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2021/NR/d1nr05708a/d1nr05708a-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">Electrochemical kinetics analysis of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode. (a) CV curves at various scan rates from 0.1 to 3.0 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>; (b) plot of log(scan rate) <span class="italic">versus</span> log(current); (c) capacitive contribution in the CV curves at scan rates of 0.6, 0.8 and 1.0 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>; (d) contribution ratio of the capacitive and diffusion-controlled charge <span class="italic">versus</span> scan rate; (e) GITT potential and (f) the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> diffusion coefficient profiles.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">The electrochemical impedances of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC and the pristine MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> electrodes were measured, as shown in the Nyquist plot in Fig. S6.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> It can be seen that the Nyquist plot for the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode has a smaller semicircle in the high frequency region as compared to that of the pristine MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> electrode, indicating that the former has a smaller charge transfer impedance (<span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small>). The <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small> values of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC and pristine MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> are 32.8 and 51.2 Ω, respectively. This can be attributed to the nitrogen-doped carbon in MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> layers, creating multiple active sites for sodiation for enhanced reaction kinetics.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit18"><sup><span class="sup_ref">18,36,39,52</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">To demonstrate the practical implementation of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC anode, an Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>F (NVPOF) cathode was added into the SIBs to complete the full-cell system for further electrochemical performance evaluation.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit53"><sup><span class="sup_ref">53</span></sup></a> The XRD pattern and SEM image of Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>F are shown in Fig. S7.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a><a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5a and b</a> show that the full cell SIBs containing MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC//NVPOF have a specific capacity of 187.5 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 50 cycles. The assembled full cell SIBs could also light up the LED cube (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5b</a>, inset), showing the realistic implementation of our innovative SIBs. The capacities of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC//NVPOF are 283.8, 251.5, 223.7, 203.8, and 184.0 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, and 2.0 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5c</a> and S8<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). After the activation process, a higher capacity of 233.9 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> can be achieved by the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC//NVPOF battery and even the current density returned to 0.1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, indicating that the SIB battery is highly reversible. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5d</a> shows a comparison of the specific power and specific energy of the as-prepared full SIBs to the other full cell SIBs in the literature.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit54"><sup><span class="sup_ref">54–58</span></sup></a> The entire SIBs can reach a high specific energy of 78.57 W h kg<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at a specific power of 193.76 W kg<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and a high specific energy of 49.70 W h kg<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at a specific power of 3756.80 W kg<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. These values are higher than those of the reported ones, making our MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC//NVPOF electrodes a promising alternative for practical implementation in SIBs.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig5"><a href="/image/article/2021/NR/d1nr05708a/d1nr05708a-f5_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d1nr05708a-f5.tif" src="/image/article/2021/NR/d1nr05708a/d1nr05708a-f5.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 5 </b> <span id="fig5"><span class="graphic_title">The energy storage performance of full cell MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC//NVPOF batteries. (a) Typical charging/discharging curves and (b) cycling stability at a current density of 0.1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> with an inset picture of a cube LED model lighted up by SIBs consisting of an MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC//NVPOF electrode. (c) Rate performances of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC//NVPOF batteries at various current densities. (d) The Ragone plot of an MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC//NVPOF full cell <span class="italic">versus</span> other reported full cell SIBs.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">To understand the role of N-doped carbon in the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC composite in the sodiation process, density functional theory (DFT) calculation was performed systematically to compare the adsorption and diffusion of Na ions on the upper surface and interlayer of the bilayer (BL)-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC by a computational approach, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6</a>. On the upper surface of BL-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, there are two stable adsorption sites for Na, namely, the top site on the Mo atom (site 1) and the hollow site (site 2), as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6a and b</a>. The calculation results indicate that Na adsorption on site 1 (0.73 eV) is more stable than that on site 2 (0.70 eV). When Na is inserted into the interlayer of BL-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, there are also two stable adsorption sites: the top site on the Mo atom (site 3) and the hollow site (site 4) (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6c and d</a>). Similarly, the top site (site 3, 1.49 eV) shows higher stability compared to the hollow site (site 4, 1.43 eV) for adsorption of Na; both are about twice as stronger as those on the corresponding surface sites. For the upper surface of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> in the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC heterostructure, the adsorption of Na ions at site 1 and site 2 is 1.44 eV and 1.41 eV, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6e and f</a>. It is evident that the existence of graphene could greatly enhance the adsorption performance of Na ions compared with BL-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>. With the inclusion of graphene, the <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ads</sub></small> of Na on the upper surface of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> could be enhanced by more than 0.7 eV, which may be attributed to the stronger interaction between MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and graphene than that between MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers. For the interface in the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC heterostructure, the adsorption of Na on site 3 and site 4 is 1.46 eV and 1.51 eV, respectively (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6g and h</a>). The adsorption strength is comparable to those on site 3 and site 4 in BL-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>. We then compared the diffusion barriers of Na ions at BL-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC. We plotted the energy diagram for Na ion diffusion from one stable adsorption site to another in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6i–l</a>. It is found that on the upper surface, the diffusion barrier of Na on MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC (0.139 eV) is fairly close to that on BL-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> (0.134 eV), showing a similar diffusion rate. However, the diffusion barrier of Na on the interface of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC (0.178 eV) will be significantly lower than that on the interlayer of BL-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> (0.204 eV). The above results indicate that the diffusion rate of Na on the interface of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC will be much faster than that on the interlayer of BL-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig6"><a href="/image/article/2021/NR/d1nr05708a/d1nr05708a-f6_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d1nr05708a-f6.tif" src="/image/article/2021/NR/d1nr05708a/d1nr05708a-f6.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 6 </b> <span id="fig6"><span class="graphic_title">The optimized structures (both top views and side views) of (a–d) Na adsorbed MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and (e–h) Na adsorbed MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC. Different adsorption sites and corresponding <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ads</sub></small> values are provided. The diffusion pathway and the energy profile of Na on (i) the upper surface of BL-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, (j) the interlayer of BL-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, (k) the upper surface of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC and (l) the interlayer of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC. All the energies are relative to the first configuration.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect744"><span class="a_heading">4. Conclusion</span></h2>
      <span>To summarize, the intercalation of the N-doped carbon layer was used to expand the interlayer spacing of lamellar MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>. The MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode exhibits excellent electrochemical performances when used as an anode material for SIBs. It has a high specific capacity of 546 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 160 cycles and a high reversible capacity of 406 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 10 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 1000 cycles. MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC//NVPOF full cell SIBs also exhibited high specific energies of 78.57 W h kg<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and 49.70 W h kg<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at specific powers of 193.76 W kg<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and 3756.80 W kg<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively. The superior electrochemical performances of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/NC electrode are attributed to its expanded interlayer architecture, which results in fast kinetics of sodiation and desodiation processes as confirmed by DFT simulation and pseudocapacitive examination. This study offers new insights into the design and materials to strengthen the ability to store sodium ions in SIBs.</span>
